<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Pig</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>

<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Pig_(disambiguation)" title="Pig (disambiguation)">Pig (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

<td colspan="2" style="text-align: left">
<ul><li><i>Sus domestica</i></li>
<li><i>Sus scrofa domesticus</i> <small><a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Linnaeus</a>, <a href="/wiki/10th_edition_of_Systema_Naturae" title="10th edition of Systema Naturae">1758</a></small></li></ul>
</td></tr>

<p>The <b>pig</b> (<i>Sus domesticus</i>), often called <b>swine</b>, <b>hog</b>, or <b>domestic</b> <b>pig</b> when distinguishing from other members of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Sus_(genus)" title="Sus (genus)">Sus</a></i>, is an <a href="/wiki/Omnivore" title="Omnivore">omnivorous</a>, <a href="/wiki/Domestication" title="Domestication">domesticated</a>, <a href="/wiki/Even-toed_ungulate" title="Even-toed ungulate">even-toed, hoofed mammal</a>. It is variously considered a <a href="/wiki/Subspecies" title="Subspecies">subspecies</a> of <i>Sus scrofa</i> (the <a href="/wiki/Wild_boar" title="Wild boar">wild boar</a> or Eurasian boar) or a distinct <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a>. The pig's head-plus-body length ranges from 0.9 to 1.8 m (3 to 6 ft), and adult pigs typically weigh between 50 and 350 kg (110 and 770 lb), with well-fed individuals even exceeding this range. The size and weight of hogs largely depends on their breed. Compared to other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artiodactyl" title="Artiodactyl">artiodactyls</a>, a pig's head is relatively long and pointed. Most even-toed ungulates are <a href="/wiki/Herbivore" title="Herbivore">herbivorous</a>, but pigs are omnivores, like their wild relative. Pigs grunt and make snorting sounds.
</p><p>When used as <a href="/wiki/Livestock" title="Livestock">livestock</a>, pigs are <a href="/wiki/Pig_farming" title="Pig farming">farmed</a> primarily for the production of meat, called <a href="/wiki/Pork" title="Pork">pork</a>. A group of pigs is called a <i>passel</i>, a <i>team</i>, or a <i>sounder</i>. The animal's bones, hide, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bristles" title="Bristles">bristles</a> are also used in products. Pigs, especially <a href="/wiki/Miniature_pig" title="Miniature pig">miniature</a> breeds, are kept <a href="/wiki/Pig#As_pets" title="Pig">as pets</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biology">Biology</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Domestic_pig_skull.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2272" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Domestic_pig_skull.jpeg/220px-Domestic_pig_skull.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Domestic_pig_skull.jpeg/330px-Domestic_pig_skull.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Domestic_pig_skull.jpeg/440px-Domestic_pig_skull.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Domestic_pig_skull.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Skull</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Su%C3%ADno_alta.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3923" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Su%C3%ADno_alta.jpg/220px-Su%C3%ADno_alta.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Su%C3%ADno_alta.jpg/330px-Su%C3%ADno_alta.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Su%C3%ADno_alta.jpg/440px-Su%C3%ADno_alta.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Su%C3%ADno_alta.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Skeleton</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pig_hand_skeleton.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3872" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Pig_hand_skeleton.jpg/220px-Pig_hand_skeleton.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Pig_hand_skeleton.jpg/330px-Pig_hand_skeleton.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Pig_hand_skeleton.jpg/440px-Pig_hand_skeleton.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pig_hand_skeleton.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bones of the foot</div></div></div>
<p>The pig typically has a large head, with a long snout which is strengthened by a special prenasal bone and a disk of <a href="/wiki/Cartilage" title="Cartilage">cartilage</a> at the tip.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ADW_Sus_scrofa_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-ADW_Sus_scrofa-2">[2]</a></sup> The snout is used to dig into the soil to find food and is a very acute sense organ. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dental_formula" title="Dental formula">dental formula</a> of adult pigs is <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; padding:0 0.1em;" title="upper dentition: Incisors.Canines.Premolars.Molars">3.1.4.3</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; padding:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;" title="lower dentition: Incisors.Canines.Premolars.Molars">3.1.4.3</span></span></span>, giving a total of 44 <a href="/wiki/Tooth" title="Tooth">teeth</a>. The rear teeth are adapted for crushing. In the male, the canine teeth can form <a href="/wiki/Tusk" title="Tusk">tusks</a>, which grow continuously and are sharpened by constantly being ground against each other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ADW_Sus_scrofa_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-ADW_Sus_scrofa-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>Four <a href="/wiki/Hoof" title="Hoof">hoofed</a> toes are on each foot, with the two larger central toes bearing most of the weight, but the outer two also being used in soft ground.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most pigs have rather a <a href="/wiki/Bristle" title="Bristle">bristled</a> sparse hair covering on their skin, although <a href="/wiki/Wool" title="Wool">woolly</a>-coated breeds such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mangalitsa" title="Mangalitsa">Mangalitsa</a> exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>Pigs possess both <a href="/wiki/Apocrine_sweat_gland" title="Apocrine sweat gland">apocrine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eccrine_sweat_gland" title="Eccrine sweat gland">eccrine sweat glands</a>, although the latter appear limited to the snout and dorsonasal areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sumena_et_al.,_(2010)_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sumena_et_al.,_(2010)-5">[5]</a></sup> Pigs, however, like other "hairless" mammals (e.g. elephants, rhinos, and mole-rats), do not use thermal sweat glands in cooling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Folk_and_Semken,_(1991)_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Folk_and_Semken,_(1991)-6">[6]</a></sup> Pigs are also less able than many other mammals to dissipate heat from wet <a href="/wiki/Mucous_membrane" title="Mucous membrane">mucous membranes</a> in the mouth through panting. Their <a href="/wiki/Thermal_neutral_zone" title="Thermal neutral zone">thermoneutral</a> zone is 16 to 22 °C (61 to 72 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> At higher temperatures, pigs lose heat by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wallowing_in_animals" title="Wallowing in animals">wallowing</a> in mud or water via evaporative cooling, although it has been suggested that wallowing may serve other functions, such as protection from sunburn, <a href="/wiki/Parasitism" title="Parasitism">ecto-parasite</a> control, and scent-marking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bracke,_(2011)_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bracke,_(2011)-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>Pigs are one of four known mammalian species which possess mutations in the <a href="/wiki/Nicotinic_acetylcholine_receptor" title="Nicotinic acetylcholine receptor">nicotinic acetylcholine receptor</a> that protect against snake venom. <a href="/wiki/Mongoose" title="Mongoose">Mongooses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Honey_badger" title="Honey badger">honey badgers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hedgehog" title="Hedgehog">hedgehogs</a>, and pigs all have modifications to the receptor pocket which prevents the <a href="/wiki/Snake_venom" title="Snake venom">snake venom</a> <a href="/wiki/Snake_venom#Neurotoxins" title="Snake venom">α-neurotoxin</a> from binding. These represent four separate, independent mutations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>Pigs have small lungs in relation to their body size, and are thus more susceptible than other domesticated animals to fatal <a href="/wiki/Bronchitis" title="Bronchitis">bronchitis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Pigs have a <a href="/wiki/Maximum_life_span" title="Maximum life span">maximum life span</a> of about 27 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid32361879_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid32361879-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetics_and_genomics">Genetics and genomics</span></h3>
<p>The genome of the pig has been sequenced and contains about 22,342 protein-coding genes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy">Taxonomy</span></h2>
<p>The pig is most often considered to be a <a href="/wiki/Subspecies" title="Subspecies">subspecies</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Wild_boar" title="Wild boar">wild boar</a>, which was given the name <i>Sus scrofa</i> by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Carl Linnaeus</a> in 1758; following from this, the formal name of the pig is <i>Sus scrofa domesticus</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gentry_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-gentry-16">[16]</a></sup> However, in 1777, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Christian_Polycarp_Erxleben" title="Johann Christian Polycarp Erxleben">Johann Christian Polycarp Erxleben</a> classified the pig as a separate <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> from the wild boar. He gave it the name <i>Sus domesticus</i>, which is still used by some taxonomists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gentry2_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-gentry2-18">[18]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/American_Society_of_Mammalogists" title="American Society of Mammalogists">American Society of Mammalogists</a> considers it a separate species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E4%B8%89%E6%98%9F%E5%A0%86-%E9%99%B6%E7%8C%AA_Sanxingdui_Pottery_Swine.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1080" data-file-width="1081" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/%E4%B8%89%E6%98%9F%E5%A0%86-%E9%99%B6%E7%8C%AA_Sanxingdui_Pottery_Swine.jpg/220px-%E4%B8%89%E6%98%9F%E5%A0%86-%E9%99%B6%E7%8C%AA_Sanxingdui_Pottery_Swine.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/%E4%B8%89%E6%98%9F%E5%A0%86-%E9%99%B6%E7%8C%AA_Sanxingdui_Pottery_Swine.jpg/330px-%E4%B8%89%E6%98%9F%E5%A0%86-%E9%99%B6%E7%8C%AA_Sanxingdui_Pottery_Swine.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/%E4%B8%89%E6%98%9F%E5%A0%86-%E9%99%B6%E7%8C%AA_Sanxingdui_Pottery_Swine.jpg/440px-%E4%B8%89%E6%98%9F%E5%A0%86-%E9%99%B6%E7%8C%AA_Sanxingdui_Pottery_Swine.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E4%B8%89%E6%98%9F%E5%A0%86-%E9%99%B6%E7%8C%AA_Sanxingdui_Pottery_Swine.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pottery Swine in <a href="/wiki/Sanxingdui" title="Sanxingdui">Sanxingdui</a> Museum, <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang dynasty</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CapitalMuseum11.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1238" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/CapitalMuseum11.jpg/220px-CapitalMuseum11.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/CapitalMuseum11.jpg/330px-CapitalMuseum11.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/CapitalMuseum11.jpg/440px-CapitalMuseum11.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CapitalMuseum11.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bronze pig sculpture, <a href="/wiki/Zhou_dynasty" title="Zhou dynasty">Zhou dynasty</a></div></div></div>
<p>Archaeological evidence suggests that pigs were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Domesticated_animal" title="Domesticated animal">domesticated</a> from wild boar in the <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Tigris" title="Tigris">Tigris</a> Basin,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (January 2018)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup> <a href="/wiki/%C3%87ay%C3%B6n%C3%BC" title="Çayönü">Çayönü</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cafer_H%C3%B6y%C3%BCk" title="Cafer Höyük">Cafer Höyük</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neval%C4%B1_%C3%87ori" title="Nevalı Çori">Nevalı Çori</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> being managed in the wild in a way similar to the way they are managed by some modern New Guineans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Remains of pigs have been dated to earlier than 11,400 years ago in <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>. Those animals must have been introduced from the mainland, which suggests domestication in the adjacent mainland by then.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Pigs were separately domesticated in China beginning 8,000 years ago, and have been one of the most important domesticated animal there ever since.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giuffra_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Giuffra-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the Near East, pig husbandry spread for the next few millennia. It reduced gradually during the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a>, as rural populations focused instead on commodity-producing livestock. It was sustained in urbanized regions, however.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> evidence from subfossil remains of teeth and jawbones of Neolithic pigs shows that the first domestic pigs in Europe had been brought from the Near East. This stimulated the domestication of local European wild boar, resulting in a third domestication event with the Near Eastern genes dying out in European pig stock. Modern domesticated pigs have involved complex exchanges, with European domesticated lines being exported, in turn, to the ancient Near East.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Historical records indicate that Asian pigs were introduced into Europe during the 18th and early 19th centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giuffra_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Giuffra-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>In August 2015, a study looked at over 100 pig genome sequences to ascertain their process of domestication, which was assumed to have been initiated by humans, involved few individuals, and relied on reproductive isolation between wild and domestic forms. The study found that the assumption of reproductive isolation with population bottlenecks was not supported. The study indicated that pigs were domesticated separately in Western Asia and China, with Western Asian pigs introduced into Europe, where they crossed with wild boar. A model that fit the data included a mixture with a now extinct <a href="/wiki/Ghost_population" title="Ghost population">ghost population</a> of wild pigs during the <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a>. The study also found that despite back-crossing with wild pigs, the genomes of domestic pigs have strong signatures of selection at DNA loci that affect behavior and morphology. The study concluded that human selection for domestic traits likely counteracted the homogenizing effect of gene flow from wild boars and created <a href="/wiki/Domestication_islands" title="Domestication islands">domestication islands</a> in the genome. The same process may also apply to other domesticated animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-frantz2015_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-frantz2015-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pennisi2015_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-pennisi2015-31">[31]</a></sup> In 2019, a study showed that the pig had arrived in Europe from the Near East 8,500 years ago. Over the next 3,000 years they then admixed with the European wild boar until their genome showed less than 5% Near Eastern ancestry, yet retained their domesticated features.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>Among the animals that the Spanish <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Columbian_Exchange" title="Columbian Exchange">introduced</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Chilo%C3%A9_Archipelago" title="Chiloé Archipelago">Chiloé Archipelago</a> in the 16th century, pigs were the most successful to adapt. The pigs benefited from abundant <a href="/wiki/Shellfish" title="Shellfish">shellfish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a> exposed by the large <a href="/wiki/Tide" title="Tide">tides</a> of the archipelago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Torrejonetal2004_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Torrejonetal2004-33">[33]</a></sup> Pigs were brought to southeastern North America from Europe by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hernando_de_Soto_(explorer)" title="Hernando de Soto (explorer)">de Soto</a> and other early Spanish <a href="/wiki/Exploration" title="Exploration">explorers</a>. Escaped pigs became <a href="/wiki/Feral_pig" title="Feral pig">feral</a> and caused a great deal of disruption to <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">Native Americans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
Feral pig populations in the southeastern United States have since migrated north and are a growing concern in the <a href="/wiki/Midwestern_United_States" title="Midwestern United States">Midwest</a>. Considered an invasive species, many state agencies have programs to trap or hunt feral pigs as means of removal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Domestic pigs have become feral in many other parts of the world (e.g. New Zealand and northern <a href="/wiki/Queensland" title="Queensland">Queensland</a>) and have caused substantial environmental damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Feral hybrids of the European wild boar with the domestic pig are also very disruptive to both environment and agriculture (among the 100 most damaging animal species),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> especially in southeastern South America from Uruguay to Brazil's <a href="/wiki/Mato_Grosso_do_Sul" title="Mato Grosso do Sul">Mato Grosso do Sul</a> and <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Paulo_(state)" title="São Paulo (state)">São Paulo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>With around 1 billion individuals alive at any time, the <a href="/wiki/Domestication" title="Domestication">domesticated</a> pig is one of the most numerous large mammals on the planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reproduction">Reproduction</span></h2>
<p>Female pigs reach sexual maturity at 3–12 months of age and come into <a href="/wiki/Estrous_cycle" title="Estrous cycle">estrus</a> every 18–24 days if they are not successfully bred. The variation in ovulation rate can be attributed to intrinsic factors such as age and genotype, as well as extrinsic factors like nutrition, environment, and the supplementation of exogenous hormones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> The gestation period averages 112–120 days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Piglets_IMG_5523.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3456" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Piglets_IMG_5523.jpg/220px-Piglets_IMG_5523.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Piglets_IMG_5523.jpg/330px-Piglets_IMG_5523.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Piglets_IMG_5523.jpg/440px-Piglets_IMG_5523.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Piglets_IMG_5523.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Piglets keeping warm together</div></div></div>
<p>Estrus lasts two to three days, and the female's displayed receptiveness to mate is known as standing heat. Standing heat is a reflexive response that is stimulated when the female is in contact with the saliva of a sexually mature boar. <a href="/wiki/Androstenol" title="Androstenol">Androstenol</a> is one of the pheromones produced in the submaxillary salivary glands of boars that will trigger the female's response.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> The female cervix contains a series of five interdigitating pads, or folds, that will hold the boar's corkscrew-shaped penis during copulation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Females have <a href="/wiki/Bicornuate_uterus" title="Bicornuate uterus">bicornuate uteruses</a> and two conceptuses must be present in both uterine horns for pregnancy to be established.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Maternal recognition of pregnancy in pigs occurs on days 11 to 12 of pregnancy and is marked by progesterone production from a functioning corpus luteum (CL).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> To avoid luteolysis by PGF2α, rescuing of the CL must occur via embryonic signaling of estradiol 17β and PGE2.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ziecik,_A._J._2018_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ziecik,_A._J._2018-54">[54]</a></sup> This signaling acts on both the endometrium and luteal tissue to prevent the regression of the CL by activation of genes that are responsible for CL maintenance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> During mid to late pregnancy, the CL relies primarily on luteinizing hormone (LH) for maintenance until parturition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ziecik,_A._J._2018_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ziecik,_A._J._2018-54">[54]</a></sup> Animal nutrition is important prior to reproduction and during gestation to ensure optimum reproductive performance is achieved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>Archeological evidence indicates that medieval European pigs farrowed, or bore a litter of piglets, once per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> By the nineteenth century, European piglets routinely double-farrowed, or bore two litters of piglets per year. It is unclear when this shift occurred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bintliff_Earle_Peebles_2008_p._305_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bintliff_Earle_Peebles_2008_p._305-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Behavior">Behavior</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yorkshire_pigs_at_animal_sanctuary.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2751" data-file-width="2751" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Yorkshire_pigs_at_animal_sanctuary.jpg/220px-Yorkshire_pigs_at_animal_sanctuary.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Yorkshire_pigs_at_animal_sanctuary.jpg/330px-Yorkshire_pigs_at_animal_sanctuary.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Yorkshire_pigs_at_animal_sanctuary.jpg/440px-Yorkshire_pigs_at_animal_sanctuary.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yorkshire_pigs_at_animal_sanctuary.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pigs in a wallow</div></div></div>
<p>In many ways pig behaviour appears to be intermediate between that of other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artiodactyls" title="Artiodactyls">artiodactyls</a> and of <a href="/wiki/Carnivora" title="Carnivora">carnivores</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CluttonBrock,_(1987)_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-CluttonBrock,_(1987)-59">[59]</a></sup> Pigs seek out the company of other pigs and often huddle to maintain physical contact, although they do not naturally form large herds. They typically live in groups of about 8–10 adult sows, some young individuals, and some single males.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because of their relative lack of sweat glands, pigs often control their body temperature using behavioural thermoregulation. <a href="/wiki/Wallowing" title="Wallowing">Wallowing</a>, which often consists of coating the body with mud, is a behaviour frequently exhibited by pigs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-61">[61]</a></sup> They do not submerge completely under the mud, but vary the depth and duration of wallowing depending on environmental conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-61">[61]</a></sup> Typically, adult pigs start wallowing once the ambient temperature is around 17–21 °C (63–70 °F). They cover themselves from head to toe in mud.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_61-2"><a href="#cite_note-:2-61">[61]</a></sup> Pigs may use mud as a sunscreen, or as a method of keeping parasites away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_61-3"><a href="#cite_note-:2-61">[61]</a></sup> Most bristled pigs will "blow their coat", meaning that they shed most of the longer, coarser stiff hair once a year, usually in spring or early summer, to prepare for the warmer months ahead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>If conditions permit, pigs feed continuously for many hours and then sleep for many hours, in contrast to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ruminants" title="Ruminants">ruminants</a> which tend to feed for a short time and then sleep for a short time. Pigs are <a href="/wiki/Omnivore" title="Omnivore">omnivorous</a>, and are highly versatile in their feeding behaviour. As they are foraging animals, they primarily eat leaves, stems, roots, fruits, and flowers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Pigs play an important role in regions where <a href="/wiki/Pig_toilet" title="Pig toilet">pig toilets</a> are employed. Pigs are highly intelligent animals,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> on par with dogs,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> and according to David DiSalvo's writing in <i><a href="/wiki/Forbes" title="Forbes">Forbes</a></i>, they are "widely considered the smartest domesticated animal in the world. Pigs have demonstrated the ability to move a cursor on a <a href="/wiki/Computer_monitor" title="Computer monitor">video screen</a> with their snouts and understand what is happening onscreen, and have learned to distinguish between the scribbles they had seen before and those they were seeing for the first time."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[a]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes2009-11-9_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes2009-11-9-71">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rooting">Rooting</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:209px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:JulianaPigletPlaytimeCropped%26Resized.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="310" data-file-width="207" decoding="async" height="310" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/03/JulianaPigletPlaytimeCropped%26Resized.gif" width="207"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:JulianaPigletPlaytimeCropped%26Resized.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Juliana piglet rooting on her sibling's belly</div></div></div>
<p>Rooting is an instinctual behavior in pigs that is characterized by a pig nudging its snout into something. Similar to a <a href="/wiki/Kneading_(cats)" title="Kneading (cats)">cat's kneading</a>, rooting is found comforting. It first happens when piglets are born to obtain their mother's milk, and can become a habitual, obsessive behavior which is most prominent in animals <a href="/wiki/Weaning" title="Weaning">weaned</a> too early.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-72">[71]</a></sup> Often, pigs will root and dig into the ground to forage for food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-72">[71]</a></sup> By means of rooting, pigs have been used to <a href="/wiki/Tillage" title="Tillage">till</a> farmland.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Rooting is known to also be used as a means of communication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_72-2"><a href="#cite_note-:3-72">[71]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nose_ring_(animals)#Pig_nose_rings" title="Nose ring (animals)">Nose rings</a> that pierce the septum of the nose discourage rooting because they make the behavior painful.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The breed known as the <a href="/wiki/Kunekune" title="Kunekune">kunekune</a> hardly ever roots, as it can sustain itself by feeding on nothing other than grass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup> Not having to root around in the soil to find underground food (e.g. tubers), it thus has evolved to, for the most part, not possess the instinct for rooting.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nest-building">Nest-building</span></h3>
<p>A behavioural characteristic of pigs which they share with carnivores is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nest-building" title="Nest-building">nest-building</a>. Sows root in the ground to create depressions and then build nests in which to give birth. First, the sow digs a depression about the size of her body. She then collects twigs and leaves, and carries these in her mouth to the depression, building them into a mound. She distributes the softer, finer material to the centre of the mound using her feet. When the mound reaches the desired height, she places large branches, up to 2 metres in length, on the surface. She enters into the mound and roots around to create a depression within the gathered material. She then gives birth in a lying position, which, again, is different from other artiodactyls, which usually give birth in a standing position.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CluttonBrock,_(1987)_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-CluttonBrock,_(1987)-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nest-building behaviour is an important part in the process of pre and post-partum maternal behaviour. Nest-building will occur during the last 24 hours before the onset of farrowing and becomes most intense during 12 to 6 hours before farrowing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-74">[73]</a></sup> Nest-building is divided into two phases: one of which is the initial phase of rooting in the ground while the second phase is the collecting, carrying and arranging of the nest material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-74">[73]</a></sup> The sow will separate from the group and seek a suitable nest site with some shelter from rain and wind that has well-drained soil. This nest-building behaviour is performed to provide the offspring with shelter, comfort, and thermoregulation. The nest will provide protection against weather and predators while keeping the piglets close to the sow and away from the rest of the herd. This ensures they do not get trampled on and that other piglets are not stealing milk from the sow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-75">[74]</a></sup> Nest-building can be influenced by internal and external stimuli. Internal hormonal changes and the completion of one nesting phase are indicators of this maternal behaviour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-75">[74]</a></sup> The onset is triggered by the rise in prolactin levels, which is caused by a decrease in progesterone and an increase in prostaglandin, while the gathering of the nest material seems to be regulated more by external stimuli such as temperature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_74-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-74">[73]</a></sup> The longer time spent on nest-building will increase pre-partum oxytocin.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nursing_and_suckling_behaviour">Nursing and suckling behaviour</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pig,_Kilcullen.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3240" data-file-width="4320" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Pig%2C_Kilcullen.jpg/220px-Pig%2C_Kilcullen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Pig%2C_Kilcullen.jpg/330px-Pig%2C_Kilcullen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Pig%2C_Kilcullen.jpg/440px-Pig%2C_Kilcullen.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pig,_Kilcullen.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sow with prominent nipples. Pigs typically have 12–14 nipples.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Two_domestic_piglets_suckling.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="2304" decoding="async" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Two_domestic_piglets_suckling.jpg/220px-Two_domestic_piglets_suckling.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Two_domestic_piglets_suckling.jpg/330px-Two_domestic_piglets_suckling.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Two_domestic_piglets_suckling.jpg/440px-Two_domestic_piglets_suckling.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Two_domestic_piglets_suckling.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Two piglets suckling</div></div></div>
<p>Pigs display complex nursing and suckling behaviour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fraser,_(1980)_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fraser,_(1980)-76">[75]</a></sup> Nursing occurs every 50–60 minutes, and the sow requires stimulation from piglets before milk let-down. Sensory inputs (vocalisation, odours from mammary and birth fluids, and hair patterns of the sow) are particularly important immediately post-birth to facilitate teat location by the piglets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rohde_Parfet_and_Gonyou,_(1991)_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rohde_Parfet_and_Gonyou,_(1991)-77">[76]</a></sup> Initially, the piglets compete for position at the udder; then the piglets massage around their respective teats with their snouts, during which time the sow grunts at slow, regular intervals. Each series of grunts varies in frequency, tone and magnitude, indicating the stages of nursing to the piglets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Algers,_(1993)_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Algers,_(1993)-78">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>The phase of competition for teats and of nosing the udder lasts for about one minute and ends when milk flow begins. In the third phase, the piglets hold the teats in their mouths and suck with slow mouth movements (one per second), and the rate of the sow's grunting increases for approximately 20 seconds. The grunt peak in the third phase of suckling does not coincide with milk ejection, but rather the release of oxytocin from the pituitary into the bloodstream.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Castren_et_al.,_(1989)_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Castren_et_al.,_(1989)-79">[78]</a></sup> Phase four coincides with the period of main milk flow (10–20 seconds) when the piglets suddenly withdraw slightly from the udder and start sucking with rapid mouth movements of about three per second. The sow grunts rapidly, lower in tone and often in quick runs of three or four, during this phase. Finally, the flow stops and so does the grunting of the sow. The piglets may then dart from teat to teat and recommence suckling with slow movements, or nosing the udder. Piglets massage and suckle the sow's teats after milk flow ceases as a way of letting the sow know their nutritional status. This helps her to regulate the amount of milk released from that teat in future sucklings. The more intense the post-feed massaging of a teat, the greater the future milk release from that teat will be.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jensen_et_al.,_(1998)_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jensen_et_al.,_(1998)-80">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Teat_order">Teat order</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PigCochon_allaitant.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/PigCochon_allaitant.jpg/220px-PigCochon_allaitant.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/PigCochon_allaitant.jpg/330px-PigCochon_allaitant.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/PigCochon_allaitant.jpg/440px-PigCochon_allaitant.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PigCochon_allaitant.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A sow with suckling piglets</div></div></div>
<p>In pigs, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dominance_(ethology)" title="Dominance (ethology)">dominance</a> hierarchies can be formed at a very early age. Piglets are highly precocious and within minutes of being born, or sometimes seconds, will attempt to suckle. The piglets are born with sharp teeth and fight to develop a teat order as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anterior" title="Anterior">anterior</a> teats produce a greater quantity of milk. Once established, this teat order remains stable with each piglet tending to feed on a particular teat or group of teats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CluttonBrock,_(1987)_59-2"><a href="#cite_note-CluttonBrock,_(1987)-59">[59]</a></sup> Stimulation of the anterior teats appears to be important in causing milk letdown,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fraser,_(1973)_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fraser,_(1973)-81">[80]</a></sup> so it might be advantageous to the entire litter to have these teats occupied by healthy piglets.
Using an artificial sow to rear groups of piglets, recognition of a teat in a particular area of the udder depended initially on visual orientation by means of reference points on the udder to find the area, and then the olfactory sense for the more accurate search within that area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jeppesen,_(1982)_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jeppesen,_(1982)-82">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Senses">Senses</span></h3>
<p>Pigs have <a href="/wiki/Panorama" title="Panorama">panoramic</a> vision of approximately 310° and <a href="/wiki/Binocular_vision" title="Binocular vision">binocular vision</a> of 35° to 50°. It is thought they have no <a href="/wiki/Accommodation_(eye)" title="Accommodation (eye)">eye accommodation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup> Other animals that have no accommodation, e.g. sheep, lift their heads to see distant objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup> The extent to which pigs have colour vision is still a source of some debate; however, the presence of <a href="/wiki/Cone_cell" title="Cone cell">cone cells</a> in the retina with two distinct wavelength sensitivities (blue and green) suggests that at least some colour vision is present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lomas_et_al.,_1998)_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lomas_et_al.,_1998)-85">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>Pigs have a well-developed sense of smell, and use is made of this in Europe where they are trained to locate underground <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Truffles" title="Truffles">truffles</a>. Olfactory rather than visual stimuli are used in the identification of other pigs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Houpt,_(1998)_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Houpt,_(1998)-86">[85]</a></sup> Hearing is also well developed, and localisation of sounds is made by moving the head. Pigs use auditory stimuli extensively as a means of communication in all social activities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gonyou,_(2001)_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gonyou,_(2001)-87">[86]</a></sup> Alarm or aversive stimuli are transmitted to other pigs not only by auditory cues but also by <a href="/wiki/Pheromone" title="Pheromone">pheromones</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vieuille-Thomas_and_Signoret,_(1992)_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vieuille-Thomas_and_Signoret,_(1992)-88">[87]</a></sup> Similarly, recognition between the sow and her piglets is by olfactory and vocal cues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jensen_and_Redbo,_(1987)_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jensen_and_Redbo,_(1987)-89">[88]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Breeds">Breeds</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_pig_breeds" title="List of pig breeds">List of pig breeds</a></div>
<p>Many <a href="/wiki/Breed" title="Breed">breeds</a> of pig exist, with different colors, shapes, and sizes. According to <a href="/wiki/The_Livestock_Conservancy" title="The Livestock Conservancy">The Livestock Conservancy</a>, as of 2016, three breeds of pig are critically rare (having a global population of fewer than 2000). They are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Choctaw_hog" title="Choctaw hog">Choctaw hog</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Mulefoot" title="Mulefoot">Mulefoot</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ossabaw_Island_hog" title="Ossabaw Island hog">Ossabaw Island hog</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[89]</a></sup> The smallest known pig breed in the world is the <a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6ttingen_minipig" title="Göttingen minipig">Göttingen minipig</a>, typically weighing about 26 kilograms (57 lb) as a healthy, full-grown adult.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[90]</a></sup>
</p>

//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/NMA.0063325_Interi%C3%B6r_fr%C3%A5n_svinhus%2C_Bj%C3%A4rka-S%C3%A4by%2C_%C3%96sterg%C3%B6tland.jpg/440px-NMA.0063325_Interi%C3%B6r_fr%C3%A5n_svinhus%2C_Bj%C3%A4rka-S%C3%A4by%2C_%C3%96sterg%C3%B6tland.jpg href="/wiki/Bj%C3%A4rka-S%C3%A4by_Castle" title="Bjärka-Säby Castle">Bjärka-Säby Castle</a>, Sweden, 1911</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pig_farm_Vampula_10.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3600" data-file-width="5400" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Pig_farm_Vampula_10.jpg/220px-Pig_farm_Vampula_10.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Pig_farm_Vampula_10.jpg/330px-Pig_farm_Vampula_10.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Pig_farm_Vampula_10.jpg/440px-Pig_farm_Vampula_10.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pig_farm_Vampula_10.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Exterior of pig farm in <a href="/wiki/Vampula" title="Vampula">Vampula</a>, Finland, 2021</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Show_Pig_(18097041013).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="667" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Show_Pig_%2818097041013%29.jpg/220px-Show_Pig_%2818097041013%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Show_Pig_%2818097041013%29.jpg/330px-Show_Pig_%2818097041013%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Show_Pig_%2818097041013%29.jpg/440px-Show_Pig_%2818097041013%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Show_Pig_(18097041013).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Large_White_pig" title="Large White pig">Large White</a>, a breed commonly used in meat production</div></div></div>
<p>When in use as livestock, the pig is mostly farmed for its meat, pork. Other food products made from pigs include pork <a href="/wiki/Sausage" title="Sausage">sausage</a> (which includes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Casing_(sausage)" title="Casing (sausage)">casings</a> that are made from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intestine" title="Intestine">intestines</a>), <a href="/wiki/Bacon" title="Bacon">bacon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gammon_(meat)" title="Gammon (meat)">gammon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ham" title="Ham">ham</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pork_rinds" title="Pork rinds">pork rinds</a>. The head of a pig can be used to make a preserved jelly called <a href="/wiki/Head_cheese" title="Head cheese">head cheese</a>, which is sometimes known as brawn. <a href="/wiki/Liver_(food)" title="Liver (food)">Liver</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chitterlings" title="Chitterlings">chitterlings</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blood_(food)" title="Blood (food)">blood</a> (for <a href="/wiki/Black_pudding" title="Black pudding">black pudding</a>), and other <a href="/wiki/Offal" title="Offal">offal</a> from pigs are also widely used for food. In some religions, such as <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, pork is a <a href="/wiki/Religious_restrictions_on_the_consumption_of_pork" title="Religious restrictions on the consumption of pork">taboo food</a>. Approximately 1.5 billion pigs are slaughtered each year for meat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>The use of <a href="/wiki/Pig_milk" title="Pig milk">pig milk</a> for human consumption does take place, but as there are certain difficulties in obtaining it, there is little commercial production.
</p><p>Livestock pigs are exhibited at <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_show" title="Agricultural show">agricultural shows</a>, judged either as <a href="/wiki/Stud_(animal)" title="Stud (animal)">stud stock</a> compared to the standard features of each pig breed, or in commercial classes where the animals are judged primarily on their suitability for slaughter to provide premium meat.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Leather" title="Leather">skin of pigs</a> is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pork_rinds" title="Pork rinds">eaten</a> and used to produce seat covers, apparel, and other items.
</p><p>In some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developing_nation" title="Developing nation">developing</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developed_nation" title="Developed nation">developed nations</a>, the pig is usually raised outdoors in yards or fields. In some areas, pigs are allowed to <a href="/wiki/Foraging" title="Foraging">forage</a> in woods where they may be taken care of by <a href="/wiki/Swineherd" title="Swineherd">swineherds</a>. In industrialized nations such as the United States, pig farming has switched from the <a href="/wiki/Sty" title="Sty">traditional pig farm</a> to large-scale <a href="/wiki/Intensive_pig_farming" title="Intensive pig farming">intensive pig farms</a>. This has resulted in lower production costs but can cause significant <a href="/wiki/Cruelty_to_animals" title="Cruelty to animals">cruelty</a> problems. As consumers have become concerned with the humane treatment of livestock, demand for pasture-raised pork in these nations has increased.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="As_pets">As pets</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mini_pig_2.jpg"><img alt="Mini pig 2.jpg" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1433" data-file-width="1754" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Mini_pig_2.jpg/220px-Mini_pig_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Mini_pig_2.jpg/330px-Mini_pig_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Mini_pig_2.jpg/440px-Mini_pig_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mini_pig_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_Pot-bellied" title="Vietnamese Pot-bellied">Vietnamese pot-bellied</a> pigs, a <a href="/wiki/Miniature_pig" title="Miniature pig">miniature breed of pig</a>, have made popular pets in the United States, beginning in the latter half of the 20th century.
</p><p>In many respects, pot-bellied pigs are desirable and entertaining pets. They are considered intelligent, gregarious, and trainable. They lack the genetic hereditary weaknesses which commonly afflict certain pedigree cat and dog breeds, are generally quite sturdy, and have a reasonably affordable diet despite requiring large quantities of food. However, they can be strong-willed, defiant, and independent pets which will sometimes defy training. They require access to an outdoor space at all times, and depending on the individual pig, may become housebroken easily or never settle indoors. While hardy, an injured or sick pig will require costly surgery or larger than average quantities of medicine than most pets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>Pigs are highly intelligent, social creatures. They are considered <a href="/wiki/Hypoallergenic" title="Hypoallergenic">hypoallergenic</a>, and are known to do quite well with people who have the usual animal allergies. Since these animals are known to have a life expectancy of 15 to 20 years, they require a long-term commitment.
</p><p>Given pigs are bred primarily as livestock and have not been bred as companion animals for very long, selective breeding for a placid or biddable temperament is not well established. Pigs have radically different psychology to dogs and exhibit fight-or-flight instincts, independent nature, and natural assertiveness which can manifest as aggression towards children and a tendency to panic and lash out with little warning. Cats generally are safe around pigs as neither species has an incentive to express aggression or fear towards the other, although dogs will view pigs as prey animals and in turn, pigs will challenge dogs for food, leading to very violent fights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vietnamese_Pot_Bellied_Pig.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="540" data-file-width="616" decoding="async" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Vietnamese_Pot_Bellied_Pig.jpg/220px-Vietnamese_Pot_Bellied_Pig.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Vietnamese_Pot_Bellied_Pig.jpg/330px-Vietnamese_Pot_Bellied_Pig.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Vietnamese_Pot_Bellied_Pig.jpg/440px-Vietnamese_Pot_Bellied_Pig.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vietnamese_Pot_Bellied_Pig.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A "Salt &amp; Pepper" miniature pig</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Care">Care</span></h3>
<p>Male and female swine that have not been de-sexed may express unwanted aggressive behavior, and are prone to developing serious health issues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup> Regular trimming of the hooves is necessary; hooves left untreated cause major pain in the pig, can create malformations in bone structure and may cause the pig to be more susceptible to fungal growth between crevices of the hoof,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup> or between the cracks in a split hoof. Male pigs, especially when left unaltered, can grow large, sharp tusks which may continue growing for years. Domestic owners may wish to keep their pigs' tusks trimmed back,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[97]</a></sup> or have them removed entirely.
</p><p>As prey animals, pigs' natural instinctive behavior causes them to have a strong fear of being picked up, resulting in the animal expressing stress through struggling and squealing, but they will usually calm down once placed back onto the ground. This instinctual fear may be lessened if the pig has been frequently held since infancy. When holding pigs, supporting them under the legs makes being held not as stressful for the animal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[98]</a></sup> Pigs need enrichment activities<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[99]</a></sup> to keep their intelligent minds occupied; if pigs get bored, they often become destructive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[100]</a></sup> As <a href="#Rooting">rooting</a> is found to be comforting, pigs kept in the house may root household objects, furniture or surfaces. While some owners are known to <a href="/wiki/Nose_ring_(animal)" title="Nose ring (animal)">pierce their pigs' noses</a> to discourage rooting behaviour, the efficacy and humaneness of this practice is questionable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[101]</a></sup> Pet pigs should be let outside daily to allow them to fulfill their natural desire of rooting around.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_human_medical_applications">In human medical applications</span></h2>
<p>Pigs, both as live animals and a source of post-mortem tissues, are one of the most valuable animal models used in biomedical research today, because of their biological, physiological, and anatomical similarities to human beings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-porcine2018Janu_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-porcine2018Janu-103">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-porcine2018Janu2_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-porcine2018Janu2-104">[103]</a></sup> For instance, human skin is very similar to the pigskin, therefore pigskin has been used in many preclinical studies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-porcine2018Janu_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-porcine2018Janu-103">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-porcine2018Janu2_104-1"><a href="#cite_note-porcine2018Janu2-104">[103]</a></sup> Porcine are used in finding treatments, cures for diseases, xenotransplantation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[104]</a></sup> and for general education. They are also used in the development of medical instruments and devices, surgical techniques and instrumentation, and FDA-approved research. These animals contribute to the <a href="/wiki/Three_Rs_(animal_research)" title="Three Rs (animal research)">reduction methods for animal research</a>, as they supply more information from fewer animals used, for a lower cost.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Xenotransplantation">Xenotransplantation</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Xenotransplantation" title="Xenotransplantation">Xenotransplantation</a></div>
<p>Pigs are currently thought to be the best non-human candidates for <a href="/wiki/Organ_donation" title="Organ donation">organ donation</a> to humans, and to date they are the only animal that has successfully donated an organ to a human body. The first successful donation of a non-human organ to a human body was conducted on 15 September 2021, when a kidney from a pig was transplanted to a brain-dead human and immediately started functioning similarly to a human kidney.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[106]</a></sup> The procedure, led by Dr. <a href="/wiki/Robert_Montgomery_(physician)" title="Robert Montgomery (physician)">Robert Montgomery</a>, used a donor pig that was genetically engineered to not have a specific carbohydrate that the human body considers a threat–<a href="/wiki/Galactose-alpha-1,3-galactose" title="Galactose-alpha-1,3-galactose">Galactose-alpha-1,3-galactose</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup> This followed an earlier major breakthrough when the carbohydrate was removed from genetically engineered mice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-latemple_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-latemple-109">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>Besides similarity between pig and human organs, pigs are among the best animals suited for human donation due the lower risk of cross-species disease transmission. This is caused by pigs' increased <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetics" title="Phylogenetics">phylogenetic</a> distance from humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dooldeniya2003_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dooldeniya2003-110">[109]</a></sup> Furthermore, they are readily available, and new infectious agents are less likely since they have been in close contact with humans through domestication for many generations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor2007_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor2007-111">[110]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some obstacles to successful organ donation from a pig to a human arise from the response of the recipient's <a href="/wiki/Immune_system" title="Immune system">immune system</a>—generally more extreme than in <a href="/wiki/Allotransplantation" title="Allotransplantation">allotransplantations</a>, ultimately results in rejection of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xenograft" title="Xenograft">xenograft</a>, and in some cases results in the death of the recipient—including hyperacute <a href="/wiki/Transplant_rejection" title="Transplant rejection">rejection</a>, acute vascular rejection, cellular rejection, and chronic rejection.
</p><p>Examples of viruses carried by pigs include porcine <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herpesvirus" title="Herpesvirus">herpesvirus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rotavirus" title="Rotavirus">rotavirus</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parvovirus" title="Parvovirus">parvovirus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Circovirus" title="Circovirus">circovirus</a>. Of particular concern are PERVs (porcine endogenous retroviruses), vertically transmitted viruses that embed in swine genomes. The risks with xenosis are twofold, as not only could the individual become infected, but a novel infection could initiate an epidemic in the human population. Because of this risk, the FDA has suggested any recipients of xenotransplants shall be closely monitored for the remainder of their life, and quarantined if they show signs of xenosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FDA2006_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FDA2006-112">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p>Pig cells have been engineered to inactivate all 62 PERVs in the genome using <a href="/wiki/CRISPR#Editing" title="CRISPR">CRISPR Cas9 genome editing</a> technology, and eliminated infection from the pig to human cells in culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Folklore">Folklore</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pigs_in_culture" title="Pigs in culture">Pigs in culture</a></div>
<p>In the belief of traditional Irish fishermen, the pig is seen as a thing of bad luck and should not be mentioned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[113]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Glossary_of_terms">Glossary of terms</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Pig_farming#Terminology" title="Pig farming">Pig farming § Terminology</a></div>
<p>Because the pig is a major <a href="/wiki/List_of_domesticated_animals" title="List of domesticated animals">domesticated animal</a>, English has many terms unique to the species:
</p>
<ul><li><i>barrow</i> – a castrated male swine<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup></li>
<li><i>boar</i> – a mature male swine; often a wild or <a href="/wiki/Feral_pig" title="Feral pig">feral</a> swine<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[115]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/boneen" title="wikt:boneen">boneen</a></i> – a very young pig (Ireland)</li>
<li><i>farrow</i> (noun) – a litter of piglets</li>
<li><i>farrow</i> (verb) – to give birth to piglets<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[116]</a></sup></li>
<li><i>gilt</i> – a female pig that has never been pregnant or is pregnant for the first time<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[117]</a></sup></li>
<li><i>hog</i> – a domestic swine, especially a fully-grown specimen</li>
<li><i>parcel</i> – collective noun for pigs</li>
<li><i>pig</i> – strictly, an immature swine; more generally, any swine, especially of the domestic variety</li>
<li><i>piglet</i> – a very young pig<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[118]</a></sup></li>
<li><i>queen</i> – a female pig that has never been mated</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Savaging" title="Savaging">savaging</a></i> – the act of a sow attacking her own piglets, sometimes killing and cannibalising them</li>
<li><i>shoat</i> – a young pig, especially one that has been weaned</li>
<li><i>sounder</i> – collective noun for pigs</li>
<li><i>sow</i> – a mature female swine<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[119]</a></sup></li>
<li><i>swine</i> (singular and plural) – hogs collectively or generally; also a <a href="/wiki/Animal_epithet" title="Animal epithet">derogatory epithet</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[120]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Swineherd" title="Swineherd">swineherd</a></i> – one who tends to swine raised as livestock; a <a href="/wiki/Pig_farming" title="Pig farming">pig farmer</a></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2>

<li id="cite_note-porcine2018Janu-103"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-porcine2018Janu_103-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-porcine2018Janu_103-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1" id="CITEREFHerron2009">Herron, Alan J. (5 December 2009). <a class="external text" href="http://www.ivis.org/proceedings/acvp/2009/Herron.pdf?LA=1" rel="nofollow">"Pigs as Dermatologic Models of Human Skin Disease"</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>. <i>ivis.org</i>. DVM Center for Comparative Medicine and Department of Pathology Baylor College of Medicine Houston, Texas<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">27 January</span> 2018</span>. <q>pig skin has been shown to be the most similar to human skin. Pigskin is structurally similar to the human epidermal and dermal-epidermal thickness ratios. Pigs and humans have similar hair follicle and blood vessel patterns in the skin. Biochemically, pigs contain dermal collagen and elastic content that is more similar to humans than other laboratory animals. Finally, pigs have similar physical and molecular responses to various growth factors.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=ivis.org&amp;rft.atitle=Pigs+as+Dermatologic+Models+of+Human+Skin+Disease&amp;rft.date=2009-12-05&amp;rft.aulast=Herron&amp;rft.aufirst=Alan+J.&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.ivis.org%2Fproceedings%2Facvp%2F2009%2FHerron.pdf%3FLA%3D1&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APig"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-porcine2018Janu2-104"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-porcine2018Janu2_104-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-porcine2018Janu2_104-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1" id="CITEREFLiuKimMadsenBouchard">Liu, J.; Kim, L.; Madsen, T.; Bouchard, G. <li id="cite_note-116"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-116">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Dictionary_of_Agriculture_2006_reference">Dictionary of Agriculture (2006)</a>, "boar," 30. "<i>noun</i> a male uncastrated pig"</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-117"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-117">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Dictionary_of_Agriculture_2006_reference">Dictionary of Agriculture (2006)</a>, "farrowing," 97. "<i>noun</i> the act of giving birth to piglets"</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-118"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-118">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Dictionary_of_Agriculture_2006_reference">Dictionary of Agriculture (2006)</a>, "gilt," 97. "<i>noun</i> a young female pig"</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-119"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-119">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Dictionary_of_Agriculture_2006_reference">Dictionary of Agriculture (2006)</a>, "piglet," 189. "<i>noun</i> a young pig"</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-120"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-120">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Dictionary_of_Agriculture_2006_reference">Dictionary of Agriculture (2006)</a>, "sow," 229. "<i>noun</i> a female pig"</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-121"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-121">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Dictionary_of_Agriculture_2006_reference">Dictionary of Agriculture (2006)</a>, "swine," 240. "<i>noun</i> a collective term for pigs"</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
